mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>E</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>CuS</mi><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mi>Na</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>CuS</mi><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>where <i>E</i><sub>CuSe<sub>2</sub>+Na</sub> is the total energy of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> supercell with the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalated, whereas <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>CuSe<sub>2</sub></sub> represent the total energies of the single Na atom and the pure supercell, respectively. A climbing image nudged elastic band (CINEB) was employed to analyze the energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> migration paths between interstitial positions in the lattice to reveal the kinetics of the electrochemical intercalation reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a></div></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To understand the potential of Na<sup>+</sup>-storage behavior of CuSe<sub>2</sub> materials, we performed DFT computations to calculate the diffusion energy barriers with the aim of evaluating the capability of Na<sup>+</sup> migrating through different crystal phases, e.g., typical cubic-phase CuSe<sub>2</sub> and orthorhombic-phase CuSe<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). Generally speaking, the diffusion energy barrier in CuSe<sub>2</sub> was associated with the interaction force between Na<sup>+</sup>, Cu atoms, and Se atoms. For a cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub> unit cell, there is relatively weak interaction with nearby Se and Cu atoms along the diffusion path, leading to more easy migration of Na<sup>+</sup> through it. As expected, when Na<sup>+</sup> migrated between Cu–Se layers along the trajectories, the cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub> exhibited a lower diffusion energy barrier of 2.96 eV than that of the orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> (3.61 eV), indicating the excellent mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> when it migrated through the lattice of cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub>. The divergent diffusibility of Na<sup>+</sup> between cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub> and orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> is dominantly caused by the totally different surrounding in the crystal cell. Considering that nanosized particles have great advantages in enhancing dynamics, combined with DFT calculation results, we plan to synthesize cubic-phase CuSe<sub>2</sub> with a perfect crystal structure for anode materials of SIBs, targeting high ICE, ultrahigh rate, and long-life sodium storage.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Na migration paths of (a) CuSe<sub>2</sub> cubic and (b) CuSe<sub>2</sub> orthorhombic. (c) Calculated migration energy barriers of the single Na<sup>+</sup> along the described paths.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In this work, cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals (CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs) were prepared according to morphological regulation and improvement on the basis of the published literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the detailed synthesis procedure for CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs. Here, CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and selenium powders served as copper and Se resources, respectively, accompanied by TEG and TETA as reducing agents. Upon processing, hydroxy groups in TEG reduced selenium to yield active Se<sub>2</sub><sup>2–</sup>, reacting with a Cu ion to form CuSe<sub>2</sub>. It is worth noting that water-bath quenching was used to bring the solution down to room temperature when the reaction time was over for gathering final CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs. For comparison, the bulk orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> was synthesized using a Cu<sub>2</sub>O template (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, (b) XRD pattern, (c) Raman spectrum, and XPS spectra of (d) Cu 2p and (e) Se 3d of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), all of the XRD peaks of the sample could be correspondingly indexed to cubic CuSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS no. 71-0047) without any obvious impurities, being indicative of the successful synthesis of target samples. The control sample of bulk-CuSe<sub>2</sub> was also found to be a pure orthorhombic phase (JCPDS no. 19-0400 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>). Raman spectra gave information about the chemical bond vibrations of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, e.g., the peak located at 257.7 cm<sup>–1</sup> pointing to the stretching vibration of the Cu–Se bond (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Further evidence of the chemical bond in CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs was collected from the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>d,e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d shows the Cu 2p XPS spectrum of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, where two peaks with binding energies of 952 and 932 eV were observed, which could be assigned to the Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> respectively. In the spectrum of Se 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e), the two obvious peaks were fitted at 55.1 and 53.8 eV, which were ascribed to Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">The microstructure of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs was further investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM), which clearly showed that CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs possessed a fairly smooth surface and a regular cubic feature, with average particle sizes ranging from 160 to 250 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs exhibited lattice fringes with an interlayer spacing of 0.267 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d), indexing to the (210) plane of CuSe<sub>2</sub>. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e, a set of well-arranged diffraction spots in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern were matched well with the (010), (210), and (200) planes of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, explicitly suggesting the single-crystalline nature of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs. Furthermore, the elemental mapping images proved the uniform distribution of the Cu and Se elements throughout CuSe<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f–h). Taken together, the uniform cubic-shaped CuSe<sub>2</sub> nanocrystallines have been successfully synthesized via a convenient method.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b) SEM images, (c) TEM image, (d) HRTEM image, (e) SEAD pattern, and (f–h) elemental mapping images of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs as an anode of SIBs was examined in a half-cell with a voltage range of 0.3–3.0 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a shows the initial three cyclic voltammogram (CV) profiles of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Two reduction peaks at 1.93 and 1.64 V appeared in the first cathodic scan, which correspond to the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film as well as intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into CuSe<sub>2</sub>, and further sodiation to 1.24 and 1.08 V revealed the formation of NaCuSe and Na<sub>2</sub>Se phases. The peak that emerged at 0.67 V, which intensified in the subsequent scans, is attributed to the conversion of NaCuSe into metallic Cu and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. Following the cathodic scan is the anodic scan, where an obviously strong peak of 1.52 V could be considered as the consequence of the conversion of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Cu to NaCuSe, emerging with an overpotential of 0.85 V between it and its counterpart of the cathodic peak of 0.67 V, which is also the typical characteristic of conversion reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The sharp oxidation peak at 1.99 V is ascribed to the generation of a Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se structure with lattice vacancy, which should be also discussed in the following ex situ XRD section in detail. The CV curves of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs nearly overlap after the first cycle, manifesting their superior reversibility and outstanding cycling performance as utilized in SIBs, which was also verified by the almost completely overlapped galvanostatic charge/discharge curves after the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). The CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode delivered an initial discharge capacity of 465 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a charge capacity of 463 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 99.6% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting information) because the extremely perfectly single-crystal structure led to fewer side reactions among the crystal lattice, surface, and interface of energy-storage host concerning conductive agent, adhesive, and ether electrolyte. At present, the energy density of sodium-ion batteries is fairly low, which weakens their competitiveness with other ion batteries, such as lithium-ion batteries. In this study, ICE of electrodes close to 100%, being rarely in ion batteries, will greatly improve the energy density of the sodium-ion full cell and increase its application scenarios, especially those sensitive to energy density.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs: (a) Initial three CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.3–3.0 V, (b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves and (c) cycling performance at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (d) rate performance at various current densities, (e) comparison of the rate performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and other CuSe-based materials reported in the literature, (f) cycling performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (g) comparison of the long-term cycling performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and other CuSe-based materials reported in the literature.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c shows the cycle performance and corresponding Coulombic efficiency of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC anode for SIBs over 800 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The electrode afforded an increased capacity upon cycling, which may be ascribed to the activation process originating from phase transition. After 800 cycles, a high reversible capacity of 554 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> remained and a nearly 100% high Coulombic efficiency after the initial few cycles. Besides their remarkable cycling performance, CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs afforded excellent rate performances against current shock at diverse current densities. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs rendered high reversible capacities of 393, 377, 369, 362, 359, 356, 353, and 349 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.5, 2, 5, 10, 15, 20, 30, and 40 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at an ultrahigh current density of 50 A g<sup>–1</sup>, they still possessed a robust capability to achieve a great reversible capacity of 344 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating their impressively enhanced kinetics. Subsequently, the reversible capacity succeeded in going back to 392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> as the current density lowered to 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The rate performance of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs with a higher mass loading is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. To the best of our knowledge, such excellent rate performance of copper-selenide materials as the SIB anode has never been reported before, compared with some copper-selenide materials reported in the literature, such as Cu<sub>2</sub>Se@PPy, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> CPL-CuSe, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> CuSe nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Cu<sub>2</sub>Se<sup>–</sup>CTAB, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">With the intention of further assessing the cycling feature of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode under a highly powered and long-term environment, the CuSe<sub>2</sub> electrode was conducted at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 1700 cycles. Remarkably, the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode delivered a high capacity of 380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f), superior to that of the control sample of bulk orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> with an ICE of 91% and 329 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for 800 cycles. Even at a higher mass loading, the cycling performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> did not decrease significantly (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). To highlight the outstanding ultralong lifespan of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, the specific capacities of other reported metal selenides at different current densities are summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> in the Supporting Information. The CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode demonstrated an undisputable advantage in highly reversible capacity for 1700 cycles at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> over other materials, further corroborating its promising prospect of durable practical application in an ultra-fast-charged environment. Until now, the low migration energy barrier of the cubic phase, the short path for ion transmission caused by the nano-metered size, and few side reactions originating from the perfect single-crystal structure could partly explain such excellent electrochemical performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs for sodium storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further elucidate the kinetics root of the superior sodium-storage performance of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, CV curves were measured at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.3 and 3 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). Similar profiles were found in the CV curves under different sweep rates, and the function relation formula between the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>, mA) and sweep rate (ν, mV s<sup>–1</sup>) was depicted as follows: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a><i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = <i>a</i>ν<sup><i>b</i></sup>, i.e., ln <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = <i>b</i> ln ν + ln <i>a</i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are two parameters, and the <i>b</i> value could be calculated by the slope of the line. When the <i>b</i> value approaches 0.5 or 1, the electrochemical sodiation process is dominated by ion diffusion or pseudocapacitive behavior, respectively. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b, the calculated <i>b</i> values of peaks 1 and 2 are 0.75 and 0.81, respectively, demonstrating the emergence of a hybrid-controlled energy-storage mechanism for storing sodium ions. According to the reported literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> the reduced particles’ size induced an electrochemical process to show more capacitive behavior because of more surface/interface reactions. In addition, CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs possessed a larger specific surface area than bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), providing more reaction sites. For more accurate consideration, the percentage of pseudocapacitive contribution capacity could be assessed by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>: <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1<i>/</i>2</sup>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> correspond to capacitive contribution and diffusion-limited contribution, respectively. Based on the integration of the CV curve, the detailed pseudocapacitive contribution of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC is 83.0% at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, as determined by the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> constants (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). The proportion of pseudocapacitive contribution increased with increasing scanning rate, such as the ratios of the pseudocapacitive contribution of 87.3, 89.6, 93.4, and 96.0% at the scan rate ranging from 0.3 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>). Such a fact confirmed that pseudocapacitive behavior established itself as a dominant role during sodiation/desodiation of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs with a unique single-crystal structure, leading to fast Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction, which gave a satisfactory explanation in the above-mentioned extremely fast charge storage for the corresponding electrode.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves at various scan rates after 50 cycles under 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, (b) linear relationship of ln(<i>i</i>) vs ln(ν) (<i>i</i>, peak current; ν, scan rate) calculated from CV curves, (c) pseudocapacitive contribution (orange shadow area) to total capacity at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (d) proportion of pseudocapacitive contribution at various scan rates, (e) EIS profiles of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles, (f) EIS profiles of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs after different cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (g) GITT curves of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient as a function of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub> at (h) discharged and (i) charged states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further analyze the kinetics behavior of samples, the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were collected after various cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Compared with bulk orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes, the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode exhibited an obviously much smaller semicircle radius and a larger slope after 50 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e). Charge-transfer resistance of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values tended to decrease, and line slopes gradually increased for the electrode with the cycle number increasing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f). The interfacial impedance decreases in the first 500 cycles due to activation of the electrode material and then becomes stable after 500 cycles, demonstrating the stable solid electrolyte interface layer on the CuSe<sub>2</sub> electrode upon cycling, which should be responsible for the stable cycling performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also used to evaluate the polarization and the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) of the electrodes of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs after 50 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> with a pulse current at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 4 min between rest intervals of 30 min (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). Bulk orthorhombic CuSe<sub>2</sub> acted as a control sample. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) can be estimated using Fick’s second law with the following equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref46" aria-label="reference 7 and 46">(7,46)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><mi>S</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mstyle></mrow></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where τ is the current pulse time (s) and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub>, and <i>S</i> are mass, molar mass, molar volume, and electrode area, respectively. Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> represent the steady-state voltage change after the current pulse and the voltage variation during the current pulse, excluding the <i>IR</i>-drop, respectively, which can be obtained by a cycle unit, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>. The average ion diffusion coefficients of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs were 3.08 × 10<sup>–10</sup> and 6.26 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in the sodiation and desodiation states, respectively, which were larger than those of bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub>, i.e., 1.69 × 10<sup>–10</sup> and 5.35 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h–i). The high ion diffusion coefficients revealed that Na<sup>+</sup> diffused much more easily in the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, in agreement with the result of DFT, producing high-rate performance in the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode. The above-mentioned facts illustrated that nanomaterials of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs, possessing a short diffusion path, not only could effectively enhance the mutual contact between electrodes and electrolytes but also produced accelerated electrochemical reaction kinetics after continuous sodiation/desodiation.</div><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ XRD measurement is a powerful tool to track the crystal phase transition of electrode materials during sodiation/desodiation, being conducive to finding the deep material root of energy storage, and was here conducted to investigate the possible reaction mechanism of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs during the first and second sodiation/desodiation between 0.3 to 3 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>). During the initial cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,b), the peaks corresponding to the (200), (210), and (211) planes of CuSe<sub>2</sub> are attenuated gradually at the voltage window of 1.6–1.4 V, indicating the gradual insertion of sodium ions into the interlayer of CuSe<sub>2</sub> to make a large layer spacing. When discharged to 1.2 V, the metastable intermediate phase of NaCuSe (JCPDS no. 71-1820) and partial Na<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS no. 23-0527) began to arise owing to partial insertion of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion into the host of CuSe<sub>2</sub>. With sodiation going on, the standard peaks of metastable NaCuSe declined to emerge, and two obvious peaks were allowed to gradually appear at about 22.6° and 37.3°, which were well indexed to Na<sub>2</sub>Se, confirming the further conversion reaction of NaCuSe with Na<sup>+</sup> to give rise to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se (∼0.3 V). Here, freshly electrochemically reacted copper might be amorphous and cease to be detected by XRD. Therefore, the phase changes of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs during the initial discharge process could be expressed as follows<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div>In the following charging process, the diffraction peaks of electroformed Na<sub>2</sub>Se are gradually weakened and finally failed to appear with the voltage increasing, accompanied by the recovery of the peaks derived from the low crystallinity intermediate phase of NaCuSe at 12.9, 25.3, 33.4, and 40.8° (∼1.8 V). Interestingly, at the first fully charged state, the fresh peaks of Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se (JCPDS no. 06-0680) emerged at 26.7° instead of reverting to the original material CuSe<sub>2</sub>, which may be caused by the generation of more electrochemically stable Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se and the slight loss of the Se element during the electrochemical reaction process. indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>Cu</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>−</mo><mi>x</mi></mrow></msub><mi>Se</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow></mtd></mtr></mtable></mstyle></mstyle></mstyle></mrow></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(10)</span></div></div>The real-time morphologies of CuSe<sub>2</sub> particles were traced to observe the evolution feature of their microstructure during different cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e–g). As expected, the initial perfect CuSe<sub>2</sub> nanocube maintained its almost unchanged appearance possessing a clear and identifiable angular line even after underdoing 1000 cycles, demonstrating that the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs could adapt the impetuous current impact and might retain electrochemical activity to produce stable ion-storage performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/medium/am2c20655_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical reactions in CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs for NIBs: (a, c) Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the first/second cycle and (b, d) ex situ XRD patterns in the first/second cycle. SEM images of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC anode after (e) 1st, (f) 500 cycles, and (g) 1000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/asset/images/large/am2c20655_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20655&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In brief, CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs rendered an almost 100% initial Coulombic efficiency, an outstanding reversible capacity of 380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1700 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and an unprecedented rate performance of 344 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 A g<sup>–1</sup> when applied in SIBs, which can be ascribed to their perfect crystal structure, low migration barrier, fast diffusion path, and stable microstructure retention.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have successfully synthesized low-cost and scalable CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs via polyol solution chemical synthesis. CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs possessed a perfect single-crystal structure and nanosize for ion diffusion. DFT calculation revealed CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs with a low diffusion energy barrier. The intercalation/conversion mechanism could be responsible for sodiation/desodiation of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs upon sodium-storage processes. The fewer side reaction enhanced kinetics, and the stable microstructure gave the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode a remarkable electrochemical performance to be used as an anode of SIBs, i.e., an almost 100% initial Coulombic efficiency, an outstanding rate capacity of 344 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a long cycle performance of 380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1700 cycles. These results demonstrated the promising potential of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs in SIBs and provided a reference for the research and development of other transitional metal selenide materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i20">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c20655?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c20655</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Synthesis, XRD pattern, and SEM images of bulk CuSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure S1); survey XPS spectrum and the SEM image of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs (Figures S2 and S3); electrochemical performance with a higher mass loading of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs (Figures S4 and S5); nitrogen isotherm plots of all samples (Figure S6); pseudocapacitive contributions at different scan rates (Figure S7); detailed voltage response of CuSe<sub>2</sub> NCs and SEM images of sample thickness (Figure S8); and comparison of the electrochemical performance of the CuSe<sub>2</sub> NC electrode with others in SIBs (Tables S1 and S2) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20655/suppl_file/am2c20655_si_001.pdf">am2c20655_si_001.pdf (734.29 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c20655" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrahigh Rate Performance of Hollow Antimony Nanoparticles Impregnated in Open Carbon Boxes for Sodium-Ion Battery under Elevated Temperature</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1903521</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrahigh rate performance of hollow antimony nanoparticles impregnated in open carbon boxes for sodium-ion battery under elevated temperature</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Anding; Xia, Qi; Zhang, Shenkui; Duan, Huanhuan; Yan, Yurong; Wu, Songping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">45</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1903521</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony is a competitive and promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high theor. capacity.  However, the poor rate capability and fast capacity fading greatly restrict its practical application.  To address the above issues, a facile and eco-friendly sacrificial template method is developed to synthesize hollow Sb nanoparticles impregnated in open carbon boxes (Sb HPs@OCB).  The as-obtained Sb HPs@OCB composite exhibits excellent sodium storage properties even when operated at an elevated temp. of 50°C, delivering a robust rate capability of 345 mAh g-1 at 16 A g-1 and rendering an outstanding reversible capacity of 187 mAh g-1 at a high rate of 10 A g-1 after 300 cycles.  Such superior electrochem. performance of the Sb HPs@OCB can be attributed to the comprehensive characteristics of improved kinetics derived from hollow Sb nanoparticles impregnated into 2D carbon nanowalls, the existence of robust Sb-O-C bond, and enhanced pseudocapacitive behavior.  All those factors enable Sb HPs@OCB great potential and distinct merit for large-scale energy storage of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr2x_05ZO4F9LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lglv8T86gUE2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvVWktrfF&amp;md5=cea3cb2430f4e0dfe9dc1e559d690941</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201809195"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multicore–Shell Bi@N-doped Carbon Nanospheres for High Power Density and Long Cycle Life Sodium- and Potassium-Ion Anodes</span>. <i>Adv.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE02836B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zachariah, M. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Extremely stable antimony-carbon composite anodes for potassium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">615</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">623</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C8EE02836B</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D615-623%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DJ.%2BZheng%26author%3DY.%2BYang%26author%3DX.%2BFan%26author%3DG.%2BJi%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DS.%2BHou%26author%3DM.%2BR.%2BZachariah%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26title%3DExtremely%2Bstable%2Bantimony-carbon%2Bcomposite%2Banodes%2Bfor%2Bpotassium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC8EE02836B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c20655&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC8EE02836B&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extremely stable antimony-carbon composite anodes for potassium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Jing; Yang, Yong; Fan, Xiulin; Ji, Guangbin; Ji, Xiao; Wang, Haiyang; Hou, Singyuk; Zachariah, Michael R.; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">615-623</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have been considered as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries due to potassium's high natural abundance of 2.09 wt% (vs. 0.0017 wt% for Li) and K/K+ having a low redox potential of -2.93 V (vs. -2.71 V for Na/Na+).  However, PIB electrodes still suffer huge challenges due to the large K-ion radius and slow reaction dynamics.  Herein, we report a high-capacity Sb@CSN composite anode with Sb nanoparticles uniformly encapsulated by a carbon sphere network (CSN) for PIBs.  First-principles computations and electrochem. characterization confirm a reversible sequential phase transformation of KSb2, KSb, K5Sb4, and K3Sb during the potassiation/depotassiation process.  In a concd. 4 M KTFSI/EC + DEC electrolyte, the Sb@CSN anode delivers a high reversible capacity of 551 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles with an extremely slow capacity decay of only 0.06% per cycle from the 10th to 100th cycle; when at a high c.d. of 200 mA g-1, the Sb@CSN anode still maintains a capacity of 504 mA h g-1 after 220 cycles.  The Sb@CSN anodes demonstrate one of the best electrochem. performances for all K-ion battery anodes reported to date.  The exceptional performance of Sb@CSN should be attributed to the efficient encapsulation of small Sb nanoparticles in the conductive carbon network as well as the formation of a robust KF-rich SEI layer on the Sb@CSN anode in the concd. 4 M KTFSI/EC + DEC electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoYTdp9SpJZJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lglv8T86gUE2A"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Optimizing the Void Size of Yolk-Shell Bi@Void@C Nanospheres for High-Power-Density Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Hai; Chen, Lin-Wei; He, Fuxiang; Zhang, Jiaqing; Feng, Yuezhan; Zhao, Lukang; Wang, Bin; He, Lixin; Zhang, Qiaobao; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">758-767</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Bi was demonstrated as a promising anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because it has high gravimetry (386 mA-h g-1) and volumetric capacity (3800 mA-h cm-3).  However, Bi suffers from large vol. expansion during sodiation, leading to poor electrochem. performance.  The construction of a nanostructure with sufficient void space to accommodate the vol. change was proven effective for achieving prolonged cycling stability.  However the excessive void space will definitely decrease the volumetric energy d. of the battery.  Herein, the authors design optimized Bi@Void@C nanospheres (Bi@Void@C-2) with yolk-shell structure that exhibit the best cycling performance and enhanced volumetric energy d.  The optimized void space not only could buffer the vol. change of the Bi nanosphere but also could keep the high volumetric energy d. of the battery.  The Bi@Void@C-2 shows an excellent rate capacity of 173 mA-h g-1 at ultrahigh c.d. of 100 A g-1 and long-cycle life (198 mA-h g-1 at 20 A g-1 over 10,000 cycles).  The origin of the superior performance is achieved through in-depth fundamental studies during battery operation using in situ XRD and in situ transmission electron microscope (TEM), complemented by theor. calcns. and ex situ TEM observation.  The rational design provides insights for anode materials with large vol. variation, esp. for conversion type and alloying type mechanism materials for batteries (i.e., Li-ion batteries, Na-ion batteries).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYnOlKB-gRV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6Q9qSGdaONw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Yolk-shelled Sb@C nanoconfined nitrogen/sulfur co-doped 3D porous carbon microspheres for sodium-ion battery anode with ultralong high-rate cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Bochao; Qin, Hongye; Li, Kai; Zhang, Biao; Liu, Enzuo; Zhao, Naiqin; Shi, Chunsheng; He, Chunnian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb materials have considered to be one of the most excellent anode materials for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Developing a Sb-based materials with high-rate long-term cycling durability is highly requisite for boosting their practical application as SIB anodes.  In this work, yolk-shell structured Sb@C nanoconfined nitrogen-sulfur co-doped 3D porous carbon microspheres (Sb@NS-3DPCMSs) were prepd. via salt-templating directed spray-drying strategy combined with ingenious and continuous high efficiency one-pot multi-step approach.  The formation mechanism of the yolk-shell structure was revealed by first-principles simulations for the first time.  In the constructed 3D architecture, the robust yolk-shell structure for confining Sb nanocrystals can provide enough void space for effectively buffering the vol. expansion of Sb and thus remarkably stabilize the structural integrity of the overall electrode during rapid long-term cycling, while the interconnected empty carbon box with abundant hierarchical pores and high cond. can facilitate the fast transport of electrons, sodium ions and electrolyte in the whole electrode.  Moreover, nitrogen-sulfur co-doping can enhance the intercalation kinetics of sodium ions, and further increase the capacity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrvBVXjL2awf7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6Q9qSGdaONw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFSqt7bM&amp;md5=490570aed05dffe02faf06facd39d792</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/smll.202001976"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.-H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational Design of Sb@C@TiO<sub>2</sub> Triple-Shell Nanoboxes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>16</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2001976</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational Design of Sb@C@TiO2 Triple-Shell Nanoboxes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kong, Ming; Liu, Yan; Zhou, Bin; Yang, Kaixuan; Tang, Jianfeng; Zhang, Ping; Zhang, Wen-Hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high theor. capacity and appropriate sodiation potential.  However, its practical application is severely impeded by its poor cycling stability caused by dramatic volumetric variations during sodium uptake and release processes.  Here, to circumvent this obstacle, Sb@C@TiO2 triple-shell nanoboxes (TSNBs) are synthesized through a template-engaged galvanic replacement approach.  The TSNB structure consists of an inner Sb hollow nanobox protected by a conductive carbon middle shell and a TiO2-nanosheet-constructed outer shell.  This structure offers dual protection to the inner Sb and enough room to accommodate vol. expansion, thus promoting the structural integrity of the electrode and the formation of a stable solid-electrolyte interface film.  Benefiting from the rational structural design and synergistic effects of Sb, carbon, and TiO2, the Sb@C@TiO2 electrode exhibits superior rate performance (212 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1) and outstanding long-term cycling stability (193 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 4000 cycles).  Moreover, a full cell assembled with a configuration of Sb@C@TiO2//Na3(VOPO4)2F displays a high output voltage of 2.8 V and a high energy d. of 179 Wh kg-1, revealing the great promise of Sb@C@TiO2 TSNBs as the electrode in SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGreTfLplDLzwrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6Q9qSGdaONw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvFWmtbjJ&amp;md5=de9e2211b9e6d06ee5f518432acb5661</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c17040"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Confining MoSe<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets into N-Doped Hollow Porous Carbon Microspheres for Fast-Charged and Long-Life Potassium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Confining MoSe2 Nanosheets into N-Doped Hollow Porous Carbon Microspheres for Fast-Charged and Long-Life Potassium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhong, Fulan; Xu, Anding; Zeng, Qi; Wang, Yijun; Li, Guilan; Xu, Zhiguang; Yan, Yurong; Wu, Songping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The potassium-ion battery (PIB) is the most promising alternative to a lithium-ion battery (LIB).  Exploitation of a suitable electrode material is crucial to promote the development of PIBs.  The MoSe2 material has attracted much attention due to its high theor. capacity, unique layered structure, and good cond.  However, the potassium storage property of MoSe2 has been suffering from structural fragmentation and sluggish reaction kinetic caused by large potassium ions upon insertion/extn., which needs to be further improved.  Herein, the MoSe2 nanosheets are confined into N-doped hollow porous carbon microspheres (MoSe2@N-HCS) by spray drying and high-temp. selenization.  It delivers a superior rate performance of 113.7 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1 and remains at a high capacity of 158.3 mAh g-1 at 2 A g-1 even after 16 700 cycles for PIBs.  The excellent electrochem. performance can be attributed to unique structure, N-doping, and robust chem. bonds.  The storage mechanism of MoSe2 for potassium ions was explored.  The outstanding properties of MoSe2@N-HCS make it a promising anode material for PIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfiZh4oyjv9LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6Q9qSGdaONw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXislSnsrzK&amp;md5=50a1bafda533d76ecd1ac425796a5b8c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201700522"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MoSe<sub>2</sub>-Covered N,P-Doped Carbon Nanosheets as a Long-Life and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Urchin-Like CoSe2 as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Kai; Park, Mihui; Zhou, Limin; Lee, Gi-Hyeok; Li, Weijie; Kang, Yong-Mook; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6728-6735</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Urchin-like CoSe2 assembled by nanorods was synthesized via simple solvothermal route and was 1st applied as an anode material for Na-ion batteries (SIBs) with ether-based electrolytes.  The CoSe2 delivers excellent sodiation and de-sodiation properties when using 1 M NaCF3SO3 in diethyleneglycol dimethylether as an electrolyte and cycling between 0.5 and 3.0 V.  A high discharge capacity of 0.410 A h g-1 is obtained at 1 A g-1 after 1800 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of 98.6% calcd. from the 30th cycle.  Even at an ultrahigh rate of 50 A g-1, the capacity still maintains 0.097 A h g-1.  The reaction mechanism of the as-prepd. CoSe2 is also studied.  At discharged 1.56 V, insertion reaction occurs, while 2 conversion reactions take place at the 2nd and 3rd plateaus around 0.98 and 0.65 V.  During the charge process, Co 1st reacts with Na2Se to form NaxCoSe2 and then turns back to CoSe2.  In addn. to Na/CoSe2 half cells, Na3V2(PO4)3/CoSe2 full cell with excessive amt. of Na3V2(PO4)3 was studied.  The full cell exhibits a reversible capacity of 0.380 Ah g-1.  This work enriches the possibilities for anode materials for SIBs with high performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpzIV0Nvfw2qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lizmVXRec4MXw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht1GhtLnM&amp;md5=5a3155a0784f8e8bcf04652fe8bd5e08</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.03.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abdelkader, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumar, R.                 10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.03.008</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D59%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D762-772%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DS.%2BLu%26author%3DT.%2BZhu%26author%3DH.%2BWu%26author%3DY.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DA.%2BAbdelkader%26author%3DK.%2BXi%26author%3DW.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DS.%2BDing%26author%3DG.%2BGao%26author%3DR.%2BV.%2BKumar%26title%3DConstruction%2Bof%2Bultrafine%2BZnSe%2Bnanoparticles%2Bon%252Fin%2Bamorphous%2Bcarbon%2Bhollow%2Bnanospheres%2Bwith%2Bhigh-power-density%2Bsodium%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2019.03.008&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c20655&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2019.03.008&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Construction of ultrafine ZnSe nanoparticles on/in amorphous carbon hollow nanospheres with high-power-density sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Shiyao; Zhu, Tianxiang; Wu, Hu; Wang, Yuankun; Li, Jiao; Abdelkader, Amr; Xi, Kai; Wang, Wei; Li, Yanguang; Ding, Shujiang; Gao, Guoxin; Kumar, R. Vasant</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">762-772</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising candidate to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the inexpensive and abundant sodium reserves.  However, the application of anode materials for SIBs still confront rapid capacity fading and undesirable rate capability.  Here we simultaneously grow ultrafine ZnSe nanoparticles on the inner walls and the outer surface of hollow carbon nanospheres (ZnSe@HCNs), giving a unique hierarchical hybrid nanostructure that can sustain a capacity of 361.9 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 over 1000 cycles and 266.5 mAh g-1 at 20 A g-1.  Our investigations indicate that the sodium storage mechanism of ZnSe@HCNs electrodes is a mixt. of alloying and conversion reactions, where ZnSe converts to Na2Se and NaZn13 through a series of intermediate compds.  Also, a full cell is constructed from our designed ZnSe@HCNs anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode.  It delivers a reversible discharge capacity of about 313.1 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g-1 with high Columbic efficiency over 98.2%.  The outstanding sodium storage of as-prepd. ZnSe@HCNs is attributed to the confinement of ZnSe structural changes both inside/outside of hollow nanospheres during the sodiation/desodiation processes.  Our work offers a promising design to enable high-power-d. electrodes for the various battery systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2lXm2W3SJvLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lizmVXRec4MXw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXlt1yqtL0%253D&amp;md5=39895f776821cb0a0b91b85a640c079f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.05.039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In-situ rooting ZnSe/N-doped hollow carbon architectures as high-rate and long-life anode materials for half/full sodium-ion and potassium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>23</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">35</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">45</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">22841</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">22850</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.8b06318</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D22841-22850%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DD.%2BLi%26author%3DJ.%2BZhou%26author%3DX.%2BChen%26author%3DH.%2BSong%26title%3DAchieving%2BUltrafast%2Band%2BStable%2BNa-Ion%2BStorage%2Bin%2BFeSe2%2BNanorods%252FGraphene%2BAnodes%2Bby%2BControlling%2Bthe%2BSurface%2BOxide%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.8b06318&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c20655&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.8b06318&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Achieving Ultrafast and Stable Na-Ion Storage in FeSe2 Nanorods/Graphene Anodes by Controlling the Surface Oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Dan; Zhou, Jisheng; Chen, Xiaohong; Song, Huaihe</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">26</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22841-22850</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designing transitional metal selenides (TMSes) with superior rate and cyclic performance for sodium-ion storage remains great challenges.  To achieve this task, the influence of surface oxides on Na-ion storage behavior of FeSe2 is investigated by designing FeSe2 with varying oxide content.  It is found that surface oxide has an inhibitory effect on the activity of FeSe2.  Small-sized FeSe2 on graphene with higher surface oxide content exhibits obviously inferior performance compared to large-sized FeSe2 with lower oxide content.  By controlling oxide content, the prepd. FeSe2 nanorods/graphene exhibits a high capacity of 459 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g and superior rate performance.  Only 10% capacity decrease occurs with the increase in c.d. from 0.1 to 5 A/g.  Even at 25 A/g (∼50 C), it delivers a capacity of 227 mAh/g with almost no decay after 800 cycles.  The influence mechanism of surface oxide is investigated.  The oxide can be converted to a sodiated shell with high mech. strength and poor cond., which generates phase-transition resistance to suppress the sodiation of FeSe2 core, blocks the transfer of Na-ions and electrons in subsequent sodiation processes.  Understanding the effect of surface oxide on Na-ion storage will be helpful in designing TMSes and other active materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaBlVnT1ymz7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lizmVXRec4MXw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtFSrsbnI&amp;md5=913bebda42e3d31c2a5910bf797009be</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/advs.202104630"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.-P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, F.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Fabrication of Cuprous Selenide Electrode via Selenization of Copper Current Collector for High-Efficiency Potassium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Fabrication of Cuprous Selenide Electrode via Selenization of Copper Current Collector for High-Efficiency Potassium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Xi; Li, Malin; Wang, Shi-Ping; Wang, Chunzhong; Shen, Zexiang; Bai, Fu-Quan; Du, Fei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Science (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2104630</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASDCCF</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Selenium-based materials are considered as desirable candidates for potassium-ion and sodium-ion storage.  Herein, an in situ fabrication method is developed to prep. an integrated cuprous selenide electrode by means of directly chem. selenization of the copper current collector with com. selenium powder.  Interestingly, only the electrolyte of 1 M potassium hexafluorophosphate dissolved in 1,2-dimethoxyethane with higher HOMO energy and lower desolvation energy facilitates the formation of polyselenide intermediates and the further selenization of the copper current collector.  Benefiting from the unique thin-film-like nanosheet morphol. and the robust structural stability of the integrated electrode, the vol. change and the loss of selenide species could be effectively restrained.  Therefore, high performance is achieved in both potassium-ion batteries (462 mA h g-1 at 2 A g-1 for 300 cycles) and sodium-ion batteries (775 mA h g-1 at 2 A g-1 for 4000 cycles).  The facile fabrication strategy paves a new direction for the design and prepn. of high-performance electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpD6I6Eo4f8bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0livO5uPbDmYkg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhtVWgtLnM&amp;md5=8e22e42eaff1477b96e27ac61c947578</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2021.134477"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yue, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Asiri, A.
